# Running a Profitable Business: Understanding Cash Flow

> The cash flow statement is one of the three primary financial statements, but because it has only been around since 1988, many traditional business analysis models don't include it. And improper cash flow can mean the death of a business. This course reviews all the important topics you need to know for proper cash flow management and analysis. Professors Jim and Kay Stice review the difference be

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow) | 1h 49m | Beginner | 171K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

#### Understanding cash flow analysis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-cash-flow-analysis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-cash-flow-analysis?u=76281980&t=1)** - Many good businesses have died a premature death because they didn't properly manage their cash flow.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-cash-flow-analysis?u=76281980&t=7)** Loans are repaid with cash, employees are paid with cash.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-cash-flow-analysis?u=76281980&t=11)** Suppliers are paid with cash, taxes are paid with cash.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-cash-flow-analysis?u=76281980&t=14)** A company can't live very long when it doesn't properly manage its cash flow to be able to repay its loans, pay its employees, pay it suppliers and pay its taxes.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-cash-flow-analysis?u=76281980&t=23)** Hi, I'm Kay Stice, I'm a professor of accounting at Brigham Young University.

> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-cash-flow-analysis?u=76281980&t=62)** - [Kay] And we will tell you about a company that is poised to break the world's record for amount of operating cash flow generated in one year.

> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-cash-flow-analysis?u=76281980&t=90)** - In short, this is an introductory course with no prior accounting knowledge necessary.

### 1. The Importance of Cash Flow Analysis

> [↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### Home Depot, 1985: Case study
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=1)** - In the 2014 fortune 500 listing of the largest companies in the United States, Home Depot is number 33.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=7)** Home Depot is, by far, the dominant home improvement retailer in the United States and in the world.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=13)** - Most people don't know that home Depot was close to death back in 1985.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=17)** We know because for many years we've been teaching a Harvard business school case about Home Depot.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=23)** The case was written by professor Krishna Palepu.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=26)** - An analysis of Home Depot's financial statements from 1985 reveals that the company's income was down, but the income drop doesn't appear to be an immediate crisis.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=35)** - Not a crisis until you look at the cash flow numbers.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=38)** During 1985, Home Depot's operations were burning through four million dollars a month.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=43)** This is the amount of cash paid to suppliers, to employees, and for other operating expenses that was in excess of the amount of cash collected from customers, four million dollars per month.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=53)** - [Man With Glasses] And there is additional bad cash flow news.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=56)** During 1985, Home Depot also burned through an average of eight million dollars per month building new stores and buying some stores from other companies.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=64)** - [Man Without Glasses] In total, Home Depot's 1985 cash burn rate was 12 million dollars a month.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=70)** Eight million dollars burned through capital spending, and four million dollars burned through daily operations.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=75)** - [Man With Glasses] Startup companies often keep track of their cash burn rate.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=79)** A startup company starts off with a pile of cash, cash from the personal savings of the founders, usually not much, cash from early investors, and maybe some cash from a local bank loan.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=88)** - [Man Without Glasses] The goal of the startup company is to start generating cash from profitable operations before burning through all of that initial pile of cash.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=96)** - [Man With Glasses] The size of that initial pile of cash, combined with the cash burn rate, gives the company founders an idea of how long they can last before they have to go back out into the streets looking for additional financing.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=108)** - In Home Depot's case, back in 1985, remember that the company was burning through cash at the rate of 12 million dollars a month.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=115)** In 1985, the company had borrowed 92 million dollars.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=118)** That was in addition to the 120 million dollars the company had borrowed the year before.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=123)** Could home Depot just keep borrowing cash forever?
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=125)** - [Man With Glasses] Well, when 1986 started, Home Depot had a cash balance of nine million dollars, with a cash burn rate of 12 million dollars per month, that left them with just three weeks of remaining cash.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=137)** - Three weeks to come up with a cashflow plan.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=139)** Three weeks with the cash burning away at three million dollars per week.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=143)** Three weeks to arrange new loans or get additional cash from investors, or three weeks to come up with a plan to slow down or even reverse this cash burn rate.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=153)** - So, what did they do?
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=155)** - Well, not so fast.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/home-depot-1985-case-study?u=76281980&t=156)** We need some background in cashflow analysis, first, before we are ready to appreciate the beauty of what the managers of Home Depot did, back in 1985, to save the company.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - [man (6), - in (2), - most (1), - an (1), - not (1)
> **Code Keywords:** case, (1)

#### Net income is not the same as operating cash flow
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=1)** - A common misconception is that net income and cash flow are the same thing.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=5)** Let's work through a simple example to demonstrate that this is not and should not be true.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=10)** Here's the example, the owners of Kamila Company would like to know how well the company performed during the most recent year.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=18)** The following four transactions occurred.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=21)** Transaction A, during the year the company signed consulting contracts totaling $30 million.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=27)** Transaction B, during the year, the company completed work on consulting contracts with a total contract price of $20 million.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=34)** Work on the remaining $10 million in contracts, 40% completed.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=39)** Transaction C, during year the company collected cash from its consulting contracts, totaling $13 million, and then transaction D on January 1st, the company purchased specialized engineering computers, and software for $15 million cash.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=55)** Now because of rapid developments in technology, these computers and software are expected to be useful for only a total of three years.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=62)** Now let's consider the following questions.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=64)** Question one, how much cash did Kamila Company generate during the year?
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=69)** Question two, what was Kamila Company's net income for the year?
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=73)** And finally, question three.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=74)** How well did Kamila Company perform during the year?
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=77)** Let's first think about question one, how much cash did Kamila Company generate during the year?
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=82)** transaction A, we signed contracts and are very happy, but we didn't collect any cash.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=89)** Transaction B, we did some work, lots of work, but we haven't been paid in cash yet.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=94)** And because we are consultants in this simple example, we didn't spend any cash, we just bought, so again, no cash flow.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=101)** Transaction C, now we collected some cash, $13 million.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=107)** Transaction D, and here we spent some cash, $15 million to buy computer hardware and software.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=112)** The cash flow calculation is simple.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=114)** We collected $13 million and we spent $15 million.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=118)** So our net cash flow is negative $2 million.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=121)** an important thing to recognize about this cash flow number, negative two million, is that this is an exact number.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=128)** There are no estimates, no uncertainty.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=131)** It's an exact number.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=132)** We can get a better measure by using some judgment to evaluate what happened during the year.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=138)** So let's look at question number two.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=140)** What was Kamila Company's net income for the year?
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=143)** Net income is designed to be a measure of economic performance.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=146)** Net income is computed as revenue minus expense.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=150)** A revenue is defined as the economic value created for the customers.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=155)** Expense is defined as the economic value consumed by the company.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=158)** So net income or revenue minus expense is the net amount of economic value created by the company in doing business revenue and expense are words owned by accounts.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=169)** We, the accountants get to define them, and this is how we do it.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=173)** So let's go back over each of the four transactions and consider whether there is any economic value created for customers, revenue or economic value consumed by the company, expense.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=183)** Transaction A, we sign contracts and are very happy, but we have not yet created any value for our customers.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=190)** So no revenue, transaction B, we created lots of economic value for our customers.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=196)** First of all, we are finished with $20 million in contracts.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=199)** That's economic value created for customers.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=201)** In addition, we have completed another 40% or $4 million worth of the remaining $10 million in contracts.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=208)** Our best estimate is that we have delivered value to our customers totally $24 million, which is the $20 million in completed contracts.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=216)** Plus $4 million, the 40% of the remaining $10 million in contracts, that's our revenue.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=222)** Transaction C, yes, we collected some cash of $13 million, but we didn't create any value for our customers.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=229)** All we did is take their cash, so no revenue.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=232)** Here you can see the essential difference between cash flow and net income.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=237)** Cash flow versus economic value created.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=240)** Transaction D we spent $50 million to buy computer hardware and software, but did we completely consume them this year?
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=248)** No, our estimate is that these items will last for three years.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=252)** So this year we only consumed one third of their value or $5 million.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=257)** That's the $15 million cost divided by three years.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=260)** Net income in this example is $19 million, which is the $24 million in revenue.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=265)** The value created for our customers and $5 million in expense, the value consumed by the company.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=271)** Cash flow information is precise and useful, but raw, undigested cashflow data are not a good measure of economic performance, as illustrated by this simple example, question number three, how well did Kamila Company perform during the year?
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=286)** Accountants must make estimates and assumptions in order to convert the raw cashflow data into a more meaningful measure of performance.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=294)** That's net income.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=295)** This process is called accrual accounting, but let's ask another question.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=300)** How will Kamila pay its employees this coming Friday, you can't pay employees with net income.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=306)** This $19 million net income number is a great measure of economic performance, but you can't deposit it in your employee's bank accounts.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=315)** And in terms of cash flow Kamila, consumed cash this year, if she wants to pay her bills, she needs to arrange some kind of financing, a loan from a bank or more cash investment from the owner's.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=327)** Net income and cash flow are not the same thing.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/net-income-is-not-the-same-as-operating-cash-flow-14332590?u=76281980&t=330)** Both measures give information about a different aspect of a company's business, and we need them both.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (6), finally, (1)
> **Definitions:** defined as (2), is an  (1), is called (1), is a  (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Speakers:** - a (1)

#### Cash flow problems kill companies
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=1)** - We told you the story of the cashflow problems of Home Depot, in 1985, we hinted at the following uncomfortable truth, cash flow problems can kill companies even when those companies have a profitable business model.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=14)** Now, how do cashflow problems arise and how can they kill a company?
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=18)** To address these two questions, let's assume that we are starting a building supply business similar to Home Depot, but we will sell to contractors and home builders, not to general retail customers, and we'll focus on opening just one store.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=32)** As we think through this process of opening our one store, let's focus on cash.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=38)** In particular, notice how long we have to spend cash until we finally start to collect cash.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=44)** So, step one.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=45)** Coming up with a business idea.

>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=63)** Step two, get the land, building and the key personnel.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=67)** In this case, our business idea is to run a building supply wholesale business.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=73)** Well, we need land upon which to build our store, and we need the store itself.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=77)** We are now up against our first cash flow problem, how to get the land and the building?
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=82)** We can invest our own cash to buy the land and the building, or we can dilute our company ownership by bringing on a partner or some shareholders who can contribute cash, or we can borrow the cash from a bank by signing a mortgage agreement, even then, the bank will insist that we put up some of our own cash as a down payment.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=100)** Or, we can lease or rent the land and building from a real estate company, they will insist that we make an initial cash payment and that we start making regular monthly payments.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=110)** If we can't make any of these arrangements, then our business is already dead because we don't have sufficient cash to get our land and building.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=118)** And we also need to start hiring a management team, these key people will probably insist on being paid in cash, or they might agree to work in exchange for receiving an ownership share of the company.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=130)** So, we might have to give up some of our ownership to solve the problem of lack of cash to pay these key people in our management team.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=138)** Step three, buy inventory.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=140)** We can't open an empty store, we have to fill it with inventory.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=144)** Our suppliers may allow us to buy the inventory on credit, but we can't wait forever to pay.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=149)** They will insist that we pay in 30 days or at most, 60 days.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=153)** And, because we're a new company, during the startup phase it is reasonable to expect that our suppliers will make us pay in 30 days or less.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=161)** They want to make sure that they get their cash, after all, they have cashflow concerns as well.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=166)** So, we buy that first batch of inventory on credit and the clock starts ticking.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=172)** We are sitting on a cash flow time bomb.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=175)** Before those 30 days elapse, we have to get the cash to pay our suppliers.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=180)** Step four, start paying our normal operating expenses.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=184)** Now, we need to hire and start paying our regular staff, the people at the inventory receiving dock, the cashiers, the aisle sales assistants and the security guards.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=195)** We need to start paying for advertising, we need to pay for electricity and insurance.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=199)** Now, we are paying out lots of cash to lots of different people and we still haven't collected any cash from our customers.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=208)** Our business could die right here, from lack of cash.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=212)** Step five, customers come, finally.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=215)** We open the doors and those beautiful customers start coming in and buying our inventory, here's the payoff, but not so fast.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=222)** Our customers, home builders and contractors are business people and are used to buying on credit, and because we are just getting started and want to be attractive to our new customers, we're going to sell on credit.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=236)** We just made our cashflow problems worse.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=238)** Customers are taking inventory off our shelves and are promising to pay us in 60 days.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=244)** We have to replace that inventory by buying more from our suppliers, and they are making us pay in 30 days.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=251)** We are going to have to pay for the initial stock of inventory and pay for the replacement inventory before we collect any cash from our own customers.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=259)** Once again, right here, we could die a cash flow death.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=263)** And in a cruel twist, the more successful we are, the worse our cash flow problems will be.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=269)** Our customers buy lots of inventory which we have to replace and pay for before our customers ever pay us.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=277)** Step six, finally, we start collecting some cash.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=280)** After 60 days elapsed, those first customers start paying us the cash they owe us.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=286)** In the meantime, we have been paying our rent, paying our employees and paying our suppliers, so our customers will pay us if we're still around, if our early cash flow problems haven't killed us already.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/cash-flow-problems-kill-companies-14332588?u=76281980&t=298)** And, notice that it really doesn't matter what kind of profit margin we are making on the sale of our inventory, if we don't manage our cash flow, we and our beautiful profit margin will die together, a cash flow death.

### 2. Overview of the Statement of Cash Flows

> [↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### Introduction to the statement of cash flows
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=1)** - The statement of cash flows has three sections.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=3)** - Don't tell me.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=5)** Okay, I don't know.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=6)** Go ahead and tell.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=6)** - Yeah, the three sections are operating activities, investing activities, and financing activities.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=12)** - Okay, which of these three sections is the most important?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=14)** - Well, the most important section of the statement of cash flows is operating activities.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=19)** The operating activities are the things that our business does every day, such as collect cash from customers, pay employees, pay suppliers for inventory purchases, pay it for electricity and so forth.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=30)** - Why is the operating activity section the most important?
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=34)** Because the first thing we want to know about a company's cash flow is one of the company's daily operations are generating cash or are using cash.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=42)** - Okay, so what's the next most important thing in the statement of cash flows?
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=46)** - The next most important thing is to compare the amount of cash generated from operating activities to the amount of cash needed for investing activities.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=53)** - All right, but what are investing activities?
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=55)** - Investing activities are the investments in the productive capacity of the business.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=59)** So buying new buildings, trucks, machines, and so forth.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=63)** - Okay, but there is no way that a company can generate enough cash from its operations to be able to pay for its investing activities.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=70)** That would be like me earning enough money for my job to be able to pay cash if I want to buy a house.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=76)** - True, most companies can't do that, but some can.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=79)** We call these companies cash cows.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=81)** A cash cow is a company that generates so much cash from its operations that it can pay cash for its new buildings, trucks, and so forth.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=89)** - Well, tell me the names of some companies that are cash cows.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=91)** - Well, you've heard of these.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=92)** Coca-Cola, Exxon Mobil, Apple, Microsoft.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/introduction-to-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14328741?u=76281980&t=96)** - Cash cows, moo.

#### Overview of the statement of cash flows
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/overview-of-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14331573?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/overview-of-the-statement-of-cash-flows-14331573?u=76281980&t=4)** The balance sheet and the income statement have been around for over 500 years.

> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=130)** No cash flow.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=131)** Depreciation is an accounting estimate of how much building and equipment was consumed through doing business this year, depreciation is not a cash flow.

> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=149)** The cash outflows for dividends are amounts paid to the owners as part of the return on their initial investment in the business.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=158)** Number 10, a machine used on the assembly line was sold for $12,000 in cash.

> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=163)** This is a cash inflow from investing activities, buying and selling buildings, machines, equipment, and land, are investing activities, investing in the productive capacity of the business.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=174)** In this case, we are uninvesting in the productive capacity of the business, we're selling of our fixed assets.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=181)** Number 11, on January 1st, the company entered into an operating lease to secure the use of a building.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=188)** The first annual lease payment of $19,000 in cash was made on January 1st.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=192)** For our purposes here, it is best to just think of an operating lease as being the same as a rental.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=198)** A rental payment is an operating activity, a routine part of our business operations.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=204)** Number 12, interest totaling $16,000 was paid in cash during the year.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=209)** I saved the controversial item until the end.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=212)** Back in 1987, when the statement of cash flows was being created, the seven people doing the creating argued about this exact item.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=220)** What kind of activity is the payment of interest?
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=223)** Some said that it's a financing activity, the payment for using borrowed amounts because cash inflows from loans or financing activities, then their repayment including interest should also be a financing activity.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=236)** That sounds convincing, everyone agrees that the repayment of the loan amount itself is a financing activity, why shouldn't the interest also be a financing activity?
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=245)** However, some of the seven said that because this is an interest expense, it's an operating activity.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=251)** Their reasoning was that all revenues and expenses by definition are operating activities.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=257)** In the end, the seven couldn't agree, so they voted.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=261)** The vote was four for operating and three for financing.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=264)** So in the United States, cash paid for interest is classified as an operating activity because of this four to three split decision vote.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=273)** Internationally, outside the United States, as of 2015, each company is allowed to decide for itself what are the classify cash paid for interest as an operating activity or a financing activity.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=285)** For our examples, we will classify cash paid for interest as an operating activity.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=290)** All right, we have identified all of the cash flows, that's step one, we've classified each as an operating investing or financing activity.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=298)** That's step two, step three is just presenting the cash flows in one report like this.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=304)** Now the net amount of cash provided or used by operating activities is the key number in a statement of cash flows.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=310)** We see in this example, it's a positive number.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=313)** It's not very much, but it's positive.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=315)** In the same way that net income is used to summarize everything in an income statement, the net cash from operating activities is the bottom line of the cashflow statement.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=323)** And that's the number you should look at first.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=326)** We also look here to see that we paid cash in investing activities, and we collected cash from financing activities.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=331)** Now take a look at this statement of cash flows.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=334)** In essence, everything you ever wanted to know about a company's current activities is summarized in this one statement, how successful were operations for the year?
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=343)** We generate some cash from operating activities.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=345)** Look at the operating activity section.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=347)** What new investments were made in property plant and equipment, look in the investing activity section, where did the money come from this year to finance all this stuff?
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/practice-categorizing-cash-flows-14327842?u=76281980&t=355)** See that in the financing activity section, if you were stuck on a desert island and could only receive a single financial statement each year by bottle, floated in on the waves, you would probably choose the cashflow statement.

#### Deducing cash flow statements

>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/deducing-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=8)** Identify the cash flows, categorize these cash flow is operating, investing, or financing, and then make the report.

> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/deducing-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=292)** The $245,000 cash paid to suppliers for inventory purchases is computed as follows.

> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/deducing-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=315)** The total cash paid to suppliers, $245,000.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/deducing-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=319)** The point of this exercise has been to demonstrate how we can use income statement and balance sheet data to deduce the amount of the operating cash flows.

>
> **Code Keywords:** let (3), this. (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - it (1)

>

>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-walmart-s-statement-of-cash-flows-14328740?u=76281980&t=150)** This $13 billion, the excess of operating cash inflow over investing cash outflow is called free cash flow.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-walmart-s-statement-of-cash-flows-14328740?u=76281980&t=158)** By the way, this is called being a cash cow.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-walmart-s-statement-of-cash-flows-14328740?u=76281980&t=161)** A cash cow is a company that generates so much cash from their day-to-day operating activities that they can pay for all of their big new stuff, their buildings, and their land, and their trucks, and everything, in cash, and they still have a bunch of cash leftover.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-walmart-s-statement-of-cash-flows-14328740?u=76281980&t=176)** In other words, these cash cows have lots of free cash flow.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-walmart-s-statement-of-cash-flows-14328740?u=76281980&t=180)** They have a cash flow statement that looks similar to this, this Walmart cashflow statement, where their operating cash flow is more than enough to pay for all of their investing activities.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-walmart-s-statement-of-cash-flows-14328740?u=76281980&t=191)** They're all generating boatloads of cash, they use a little bit of that cash to pay for their investing activities and, as in Walmart's case, they've got $13 billion left over.
>

> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-walmart-s-statement-of-cash-flows-14328740?u=76281980&t=204)** What are they going to do with this $13 billion that's left over?

### 3. Using Cash Flow Data to Make a Loan Decision

> [↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### Mortgage loan application
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=1)** - Imagine that you are a mortgage loan officer.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=3)** Your job is to evaluate each mortgage applicant to determine the likelihood that she or he will repay the mortgage loan.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=9)** - Now a key piece of evidence in each applicant's file is the financial information that's included as part of the loan application.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=16)** In fact, the need for this financial information is so fundamental to the application process that most mortgage lenders require applicants to fill out the same standard form.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=26)** - [Speaker One] The mortgage loan application form asks for information about monthly income, current and expected monthly housing expenses, existing obligations, such as credit card debt and student loans, and existing resources, such as bank accounts, mutual funds, and real estate.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=42)** - A loan officer can use this information to evaluate whether an applicant will generate enough income to make the monthly mortgage payments and continue to make the required payments on other obligations.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=53)** In fact, it is difficult to imagine how a mortgage loan officer could make an informed decision without this information.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=60)** - Gaining access to an applicant's financial information clearly helps the mortgage lender make a better loan decision.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=66)** But does the applicant receive any benefit from making these financial disclosures?
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=70)** - [Speaker Two] Well, if applicants' financial disclosures were not provided, lenders would be forced to make loan decisions in the absence of reliable information about applicants.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=79)** With greater uncertainty about applicants' ability to repay loans, a lender's risk would increase, prompting the lender to increase the interest rate charged on loans.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=89)** - In fact, in a disclosureless environment, it would benefit those applicants with sound finances to voluntarily provide full financial disclosure.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=97)** Thus, disclosure of financial information allows a lender to make better lending decisions, and also allows an applicant to reduce the lender's uncertainty, leading to lower interest rates on the loans.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=108)** - All of this financial information that you give to a lender when you apply for a mortgage helps that lender do one thing.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=115)** Forecast whether your monthly cashflow in the future will be enough for you to make your mortgage payments.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=121)** - It's all about cashflow.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/mortgage-loan-application-14329775?u=76281980&t=123)** - Baby.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - [speaker (2), - imagine (1), - now (1), - a (1), - gaining (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (7)
> **Analogies:** imagine (2), such as (2)

#### Interpreting free cash flow statements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=0)** - For a few minutes we are going to pretend that we are bankers.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=4)** We are going to look at the cash flow statements for four companies, and using those cash flow data determine to which one of the companies we should give a loan.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=13)** Now, don't worry too much about the loan.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=15)** Let's assume that it's a responsible amount for all four companies.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=18)** Also, we are not allowed to adjust the interest rate for risk.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=22)** The interest rate is the same no matter which one of the four companies receives the loan.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=26)** So, we are bankers, and we are going to analyze the cash flow statements of four companies.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=32)** Now, here's the first cash flow statement for company A.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=36)** Lots of good numbers there, but what's the most important number to look at in the statement of cash flows?
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=41)** Answer, operating cash flow.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=44)** This is the first number at which we should look when we look at a statement of cash flows.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=49)** Now, what's the most important relation in the statement of cash flows?
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=53)** Answer, the relation between the magnitude of operating cash flow and the magnitude of investing cash flow.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=58)** The combination of those two numbers is sometimes called free cash flow.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=63)** Note, there are lots of variations of free cash flow.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=67)** This is the simplest, operating and investing cash flow put together.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=71)** Now, the interpretation of the free cash flow amount is as follows, how much operating cash flow is left over after you have paid for all of your capital needs, your new equipment, your new buildings, etcetera.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=83)** These are sometimes called capital expenditures.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=86)** What we do not want to do is fall into the trap of first looking at the bottom line, the net change in cash for the year.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=93)** In this case the net change in cash is plus 20.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=96)** This is not a very informative number.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=98)** If for some reason we need to boost this number at the end of the year we can get a short-term loan, or we can delay paying our suppliers.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=104)** A number that can be manipulated so easily through short-term borrowing or through delaying the payment of your obligations is not a very good indicator of core operating performance.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=114)** So, we will start by looking at company A's operating cash flow.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=118)** We go right there, negative $400,000, ouch.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=122)** Now, it's not a crime to have negative operating cash flow.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=125)** Startup companies often have negative operating cash flow.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=127)** However, in this exercise we are bankers.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=131)** We're not speculators, we're not philanthropists.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=133)** We just look at the number.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=134)** Negative operating cash flow does not bode well with respect to company A's ability to repay a loan.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=140)** Now, let's compute company A's free cash flow.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=144)** Minus $400,000 from operations, minus $100,000 in investing activities, that's minus, or negative, $500,000.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=153)** Well, at least we can now say for sure that company A is not a cash cow.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=158)** And we also know why company A has come to us for a loan.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=161)** Company A is burning cash and definitely needs a constant stream of financing.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=166)** Notice in the financing activity section that this past year company A borrowed $150,000 and received an additional $370,000 as cash investment from shareholders.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=177)** Yes, with negative free cash flow of $500,000 company A definitely needs external financing.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=184)** Here's the uncomfortable question that we, as bankers, need to ask ourselves, how will company A repay a loan?
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=191)** Well, the only way to repay a loan is with new money from outside, a new loan or new investment from owners.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=197)** So, can we count on a new bank, perhaps a little more foolish than we are, coming along in the future to loan company A the money to pay us back?
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=205)** Hmm, so does company A need a loan?
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=208)** Yes.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=209)** I give company A the following label, Help!
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=212)** They need cash.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=214)** Based on these cash flow data I don't want to give the loan to company A.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=217)** Hopefully one of the other three companies will look better than this.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=221)** Side note, let's forget about being bankers for a moment.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=223)** Let's think big and assume that we are rich, private investors.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=228)** In that case if we think that company A has a great business idea we wouldn't loan money to company A, we would provide cash and buy partial ownership in company A.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=238)** That way if company A does well we would do well as well.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=241)** Okay, we're bankers again.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=243)** Let's look at the next company.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/interpreting-free-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=244)** I don't think we're going to give the loan to company A based on their cash flow data.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Speakers:** - for (1)

#### Relationships in cash flow statements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=8)** We looked at company A and decided probably not.
