>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=49)** That's 50,000 operating cashflow and 135,000 investing cashflow.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=54)** Well, so far, company B looks good with this sizable, positive free cash flow, but there's something strange about this company, the positive cash from investing activities, that's strange.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=65)** This means that this company is liquidating itself, selling off its buildings and equipment without spending the cash to replace them.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=73)** This is a garage sale company.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=75)** It's quite unusual to see a normal operating company with positive cash from investing activities.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=80)** My label for this company, strange.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=83)** Now in company B's defense, the company did some interesting things this past year with respect to financing cash flows, company B received new cash from investors, and apparently use that cash, along with some of the cash received from the sale of its buildings, land and equipment to pay down its loans, but companies that are selling off their productive assets are not good bets to be around for a long time in the future.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=107)** With a smaller productive asset base, can we expect company B to continue to be able to generate $50,000 in positive operating cash flow per year?
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=116)** I don't think so, company B is shrinking.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/relationships-in-cash-flow-statements?u=76281980&t=118)** I don't like company B.
>

#### Classifying a cash cow company
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=0)** - Okay, we are looking at cashflow data for four companies and using those cashflow data, determining to which one of the companies we should give a loan.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=9)** We looked at Company A and decided probably not.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=11)** Company A has negative operating cashflow and hugely negative free cashflow.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=15)** We also looked at Company B and also turned away in disgust.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=19)** Company B is liquidating, collecting cash from selling off its land, buildings, and equipment without replacing them.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=25)** We looked at Company C and discovered a normal company with positive cash from operating activities, but that positive operating cash is not enough to pay for Company C investing activities.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=35)** We are still thinking about Company C and whether the company will be able to increase its operating cashflow in the future enough to repay our loan.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=43)** But while we're doing that, let's look at the cashflow statement for Company D.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=47)** What's the most important number in the statement of cashflows?
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=50)** Answer, it's the operating cashflow.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=53)** That's always the first number we look at.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=54)** And for Company D, we see that operating cashflow is positive $350,000.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=58)** This is looking great.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=60)** What's the most important relation in the statement of cashflows?
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=63)** It's the relation between the magnitude of operating cashflow and the magnitude of investing cashflow.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=69)** We put those two numbers together and we call it free cashflow.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=72)** The free cashflow for Company D is positive $250,000.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=76)** That's the positive 350,000 operating cashflow minus the cash payments for investing activities, free cashflow $250,000.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=84)** Does Company D need a loan?
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=86)** No, this company is generating enough cash from operations to pay for all of its investing activities with plenty of cash leftover.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=93)** In fact, the first thought we have when Company D asked for a loan is, why?
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=97)** You don't need a loan.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=98)** Of course, we aren't going to say that to the representatives from Company D because we don't want to scare them away.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=103)** How will Company D repay a loan?
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=105)** Easily, with some of its free cashflow.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=108)** Ladies and gentlemen, we have found ourselves a cash cow.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=111)** And as we look at the financing activities section of the statement of cashflows, in the most recent year, we see that Company D used its free cashflow to repay large amount of past loans and to pay a sizeable cash dividend.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=124)** We are pretty confident that Company D will be able to generate the free cashflow in the future to repay any loan that we make to them.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=131)** So let's make the loan to Company D.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=133)** Remember, the most important number in this cashflow statement is operating cashflow.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=139)** The most important relation in the cashflow statement is the relation between operating cashflow and investing cashflow.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/classifying-a-cash-cow-company?u=76281980&t=145)** The sum of these two is called free cashflow.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2)
> **Definitions:** is called (1)
> **Speakers:** - okay (1)

#### Understanding the direct method
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=1)** - Let's practice using the Jim Stice operating cash flow matrix to convert income statement data into operating cash flow data.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=9)** Here are the income statement numbers for scenario one entered into the matrix.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=14)** Now are these cash flow numbers? No.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=16)** The income statement is a measure of economic performance, which is not necessarily the same as cash flow.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=22)** But we know that we can use balance sheet numbers to convert these income statement numbers into cash flow numbers.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=28)** Here are the relevant balance sheet numbers for the company in scenario one as of the end of last year and as the end of this year.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=36)** To compute operating cash flow, we will systematically review each number in the income statement and convert it into a cash flow number.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=43)** Let's start with sales.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=45)** Were all of these sales for cash?
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=47)** Well, we can look at the balance sheet to tell us.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=50)** Note that accounts receivable increased by $130 during the year.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=54)** This represents cash not collected.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=57)** So the total amount of cash collected from customers is 270.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=61)** We have used income statement and balance sheet data to deduce that cash flow number is $270.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=68)** The $400 in sales minus the $130 increase in accounts receivable gives us 270.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=75)** Now look at cost to goods sold.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=77)** Is this the amount of cash we pay to purchase the inventory that we sold during the year?
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=81)** Not necessarily.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=83)** The increase in inventory of $10 tells us that we spent extra cash to increase the amount of inventory available for sale.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=90)** The increase in accounts payable of $5 represents some accounts that we have not yet paid to suppliers.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=95)** We have preserved our cash.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=97)** A cash savings is the same as a cash inflow.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=100)** In total, we paid $255 during the year to our suppliers for inventory purchases.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=105)** That's $250 in cost of goods sold minus $10 because we've increased our inventory plus $5 because we've preserved some cash by allowing our accounts payable to increase.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=117)** Now caution, in this calculation, it's important, it's crucial to keep track of the signs.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=122)** Expenses are negatives, so negative 250 minus 10 plus five gives the cash outflow of 255.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=130)** Keep track of the signs.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=132)** Now look at depreciation.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=134)** Depreciation is a legitimate expense representing a consumption of economic resources this year.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=140)** However, depreciation does not represent a cash outflow.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=144)** Now because depreciation is subtracted in computing net income, but is not cash, then that same amount must be added back to zero out the cash flow impact.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=154)** So as you can see in the completed matrix, total operating cash flow is plus $15.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=160)** Companies have two options in terms of reporting operating cash flow, the direct method or the indirect method.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=166)** The great news is that the matrix contains all the computations for both methods.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=172)** The matrix computations can be reported in the direct method format simply by reporting the totals in the far right column with appropriate labels, like this.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=183)** So we see that cash collected from customers plus 270, cash paid to suppliers for inventory purchases minus 255, and cash paid for depreciation with a little smiley face, zero.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=195)** This direct method operating cash flow report is very easy to understand.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=199)** Notice that the numbers are taking directly from the right column in the matrix.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=204)** Now a real company wouldn't include the zero cash paid for depreciation line because cash paid for depreciation is always zero.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=212)** We put that here just to remind ourselves that depreciation is not a cash expense.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=217)** Now look carefully at this direct method presentation of operating cash flow.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=221)** Is this easy to understand?
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=222)** In other words, can a person with no accounting training, understand what they're seeing here?
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=227)** Of course they can.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=228)** Cash collected from customers, cash paid to suppliers.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=230)** This is easy. So there's good news here.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=233)** The good news, the direct method is very easy to understand.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=237)** But there's also some bad news.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-direct-method?u=76281980&t=239)** Almost no company in the world uses the direct method.

#### Understanding the indirect method
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=1)** - Let's see why the indirect method is so much better than the direct method.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=4)** I'll go back to the same matrix, the same operating cashflow calculations.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=9)** With the indirect method, we start with net income, which, of course, reflects the impact of all of the revenues and expenses.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=16)** And then we report all of the adjustments in the middle column of the matrix.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=20)** The key point is that we are using the same calculations, but just reporting the line by line changes or adjustments instead of the line by line total results.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=30)** Here's what the indirect method looks like.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=32)** We start with net income of 125.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=35)** Then we subtract the increase in accounts receivable, minus 130.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=39)** We also subtract the increase in inventory of minus 10, because those two items consume cash.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=46)** We add the increase in accounts payable of plus five, because we're preserving our cash.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=51)** And then we add back depreciation of 25, because that's not a cash expense.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=55)** It was subtracted in computing net income.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=58)** We now add it back to get back to cashflow.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=60)** The total operating cashflow, same as it was with the direct method, is plus 15.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=65)** We start with net income, which includes all of the revenues and expense line items reported in the income statement.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=71)** Note that the operating cashflow number, positive 15, is the same whether we report the matrix calculations using the direct method or the indirect method.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=80)** The direct method is easy to understand by everybody.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=84)** The indirect method is understood only by people who have studied it.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=88)** So, why do companies strongly prefer reporting using the indirect method?
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=93)** Because this method conveys so much more information.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=96)** Here are some of the things we can see in the indirect method that we don't see with the direct method.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=102)** First, A, a reconciliation of number one and number two.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=106)** The most important number in accounting is net income.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=109)** That's number one.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=111)** The second most important number in accounting, well, there may be some disagreement here, but a good candidate for number two is operating cashflow.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=119)** So what the indirect method does is it shows us the number one most important number in accounting and the number two most important number in accounting, and a summary of the reasons why these two very important numbers are different from one another.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=132)** That's what we see in the indirect method.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=134)** B, the second reason that the indirect method is so great, what were the accountants doing?
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=140)** Indirect method tells us.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=141)** Let's assume that a company's fiscal year end concludes at midnight on December 31st.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=147)** How long will it take that company's computers to compute operating cashflow?
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=151)** The computer quickly identifies all of the cash flows and then easily sorts them into operating, investing, and financing categories, less than a second.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=160)** How long until that same company is ready to release its income statement?
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=164)** Well, if it's a publicly traded company in the United States, it has two months to release its income statement.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=170)** In other countries, a publicly traded company can wait three months.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=173)** Well, what are the accountants doing for those two months?
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=176)** They are making fine-tuning accounting adjustments.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=179)** And the end result of these adjustments and assumptions is summarized right here in the indirect method report.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=185)** In this case, there are four assumptions, some more interesting and controversial than others.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=190)** So the first assumption involves accounts payable.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=192)** The accounting assumption here in the computation of net income is that we will eventually pay our suppliers that plus five, even though we haven't paid them yet.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=201)** This is probably a safe assumption.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=202)** We're assuming that we will pay our bills in the future.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=205)** Now look at the next assumption, the inventory assumption.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=208)** We purchase some inventory, but we have not yet reported that purchase as an expense, because we assume that its value will carry forward into future periods.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=216)** Usually, this is a safe assumption unless our inventory has volatile value, such as some commodities.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=223)** Now there's the depreciation assumption, actually, a collection of assumptions.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=226)** We estimate depreciation pattern, usually straight line.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=230)** We estimate or assume a salvage value and a useful life.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=233)** All of these are accounting assumptions used in computing depreciation.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=238)** Finally, there's the accounts receivable assumption.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=240)** We have not yet collected cash for $130 in sales, but the accountants are assuming that, one, the work has actually been completed and this amount has been earned.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=249)** And two, the customers will, in fact, pay in the future.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=253)** All of these are accounting assumptions.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=255)** This is what the accountants have been working on for those two months to make sure that these assumptions are reasonable.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=261)** And the auditors have reviewed all of these assumptions.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=264)** That's why it takes two months until the income statement comes out and the indirect method highlights those assumptions.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=270)** Now, see, the third reason that the indirect method is so awesome, it identifies the soft spots in net income.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=277)** Let's say that you're an analyst evaluating this company based on its reported net income of $125.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=283)** You know that net income is computed using accounting estimates.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=286)** What's the key estimate here?
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=288)** Well, here, the $130 in sales for which the cash has not yet been collected the increase in accounts receivable.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=295)** If I were an analyst in this case, I would ask the company and the company's accountants many questions about these sales.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=302)** Can you prove that you have completed this work?
>

>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=305)** Do these customers really exist?

> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=308)** Will they be able to pay?
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=310)** This is probably the most important case in the balance sheet numbers.
>

> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=318)** The beginning balance in accounts receivable is zero.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=321)** We see that this is a soft spot in computing net income that's highlighted with the indirect method.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=327)** Now, the fourth reason, D, that the indirect method is so great, guidance for cashflow management.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=333)** Let's say you are the manager of this company, and you need to improve operating cash flow.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=338)** The indirect method immediately points you to the first area to examine, collection practice.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=342)** If we could squeeze our credit customers without driving them away, we could substantially increase operating cashflow.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=350)** Now, in short, the indirect method is an awesome source of information and it is far superior to the direct method.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=356)** The good news is that the matrix the Jim Stice operating cash flow matrix, provides us with everything we need to report the direct method and the indirect method.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/understanding-the-indirect-method?u=76281980&t=365)** Now, a reminder, almost all companies in the world use the awesome indirect method.

#### Operating cash flow data as an IPO reality check
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=1)** - Here are the income statement numbers for scenario two entered into the matrix.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=5)** Now, are these cash flow numbers?
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=7)** No, the income statement number is a measure of economic performance, which is not necessarily the same as cash flow.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=14)** But we know that we can use balance sheet numbers to convert these income statement numbers into cash flow numbers.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=19)** Here are the relevant balance sheet numbers for the company in scenario two, as of the end of last year and as of the end of this year.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=28)** Now, there's a little wrinkle here in scenario two.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=30)** This company is preparing to do an initial public offering of shares early next year.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=35)** As a result, the accountants have been quite careful to make reported net income look as high as possible.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=42)** Let's review each adjustment necessary in the matrix.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=45)** First, depreciation.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=47)** Remember that depreciation was subtracted in the computation of net income, but it does not involve any cash flow.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=53)** So to adjust the numbers to be on a cash flow basis, we need to add back the amount, effectively removing depreciation from the cash flow calculation.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=62)** We add back the depreciation amount.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=64)** Now the accounts receivable adjustment.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=66)** The increase of $100 represents cash not collected from customers.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=71)** The cash is still in the customer's pockets, not ours, so we show a subtraction.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=76)** Next, the prepaid expense adjustment.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=79)** The only way to get prepaid expenses, an asset to increase, is to pay.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=83)** So the increase in prepaid expenses represents an additional cash outflow.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=88)** We show that as a subtraction.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=90)** So as you can see in the completed matrix, total operating cashflow is negative 35.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=96)** Now, a quick trick question.

>

> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=106)** That's the beauty of the matrix.

>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=117)** Let's look at what the indirect method report looks like, because this is what all companies in the world use.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=123)** We start with net income of 125.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=125)** We subtract the increase in accounts receivable of 100.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=128)** That's cash we didn't collect.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=129)** We also subtract the increase in prepaid expenses.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=132)** That represents extra cash that we had to pay to cause that prepaid amount to go up.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=136)** We add back depreciation, because that was subtracted in computing net income.

> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=141)** But it doesn't involve a cash flow, so we have to add it back here.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=143)** Total operating cash flow minus 35.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=147)** Note that we haven't done anything here, except copy down the net income and the adjustments from the middle column of the matrix.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=153)** So it appears that depreciation is a source of cash, because we are adding it here.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=158)** Is that true, is depreciation a source of cash?
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=160)** Answer?

> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=161)** No, no, no.

> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=164)** Let's illustrate this with a numerical example.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=166)** I want to really increase my operating cash flow, so I increase my depreciation by $200, from 25, up to 225.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=174)** So now in the calculation, I'm adding back 225, not 25.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=178)** Clearly, this increases my operating cash flow.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=181)** No.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=182)** If depreciation goes up by an additional 200, then net income goes down by an additional 200, from 125 down to minus 75.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=191)** Do the calculations.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=192)** Net income is now a net loss of $75.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=195)** Add back the new depreciation of 225.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=198)** And with the other adjustments, we still get operating cash flow minus $35.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=202)** Adding back depreciation merely removes the non-cash depreciation amount, whatever it is, from the calculation of operating cash flow.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=210)** The depreciation is subtracted in computing net income, but is added right back in computing operating cashflow.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=217)** Now repeat after me the following statement three times.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=219)** Depreciation is not a source of cash.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=223)** Depreciation is not a source of cash.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=225)** Depreciation is not a source of cash.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=228)** Now, note, this statement is true as long as we are not talking about income taxes.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=233)** Depreciation does reduce the amount of income taxes that we pay.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=236)** So in that sense, depreciation is a source of income tax cash outflow savings.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=242)** Alright, now let's use this indirect method report to do some analysis.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=246)** The fact is that operating cashflow is minus 35.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=250)** No assumptions involved here, this is a fact.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=252)** So how does the company get away with reporting net income of positive 125 when the fact of operating cashflow is minus 35?
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=259)** This great net income is the result of accounting assumptions.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=263)** The accountants have assumed that $100 in sales for which no cash has yet been collected are real sales.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=269)** They have assumed that the work has been done and that the cash will be collected in the future.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=273)** The actual amount of cash spent for administrative expenses is $335.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=278)** How can the accountants get away with reporting only 250 as an expense in the income statement?
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=283)** They assumed that the additional $85 will benefit future periods.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=289)** In a situation such as this, in which the reporting company has a big incentive to report high net income and upcoming IPO, a big bank loan application, et cetera, the operating cash flow number can serve as a reality check to confirm whether the net income number is reasonable.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=305)** If I were an analyst in this case, I would press the company to confirm the reasonableness of the assumptions about the uncollected sales amount, the $100, and the $85 in administrative expenses that are claimed to be benefiting a future period.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/operating-cash-flow-data-as-an-ipo-reality-check-14331571?u=76281980&t=318)** The indirect method highlights these important assumptions.

> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=165)** Why are we adding back the loss?
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=167)** It removes it from the calculation.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=168)** We subtracted the loss in computing the net loss reported for the year.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=172)** Now we have to add it back to remove it.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=174)** So the cashflow effect is zero.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=176)** Similarly, we also add back depreciation.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=178)** It's a non-cash item, the total, plus 390 operating cashflow.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=182)** Now two questions, can a normal person with no experience with the statement of cash flows understand this indirect method report?
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=191)** No, adding the loss, they wouldn't understand.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=195)** By the way, if a gain had been reported on the sale of the building, that gain would have been subtracted here to remove its effect from the operating cashflow calculations, that net loss of $375 confirms that this company had a bad year this year, but is this company going to have difficulty paying its employees and making it scheduled loan payments?
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=215)** This situation illustrates why we need both the income statement and the statement of cash flows.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=220)** Economically, the company had a horrible year as reflected in the net loss of $375.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=226)** The income statement is a report of economic performance.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=229)** On the other hand, from a cashflow standpoint, the company's okay, how do you pay your employees, not with net income or net loss in this case, but with cash.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=238)** And in this case, there's plenty of cash.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=241)** In cases such as this one, in which companies have large losses, often from asset write downs.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/identifying-economic-performance?u=76281980&t=247)** It is important to look at operating cashflow to see if the company has sufficient cash flow to continue functioning in a short term.

> **CLI Commands:** make (1)

### 5. Managing Cash Flow

#### The Home Depot story revisited
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=0)** - When last we spoke about Home Depot back in 1985, cash flow problems had pushed the company to the wall.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=7)** - In fact, with a cash burn rate of $3 million per week, and with just $9 million cash in the bank, Home Depot had just three weeks to figure out how to either get some additional external financing or reduce, dramatically, its cash burn rate.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=22)** - So, what'd they do?
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=23)** - Well, a combination of factors, really.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=25)** But much of what they did was focused on improving their operating cash flow.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=29)** - First and foremost, they launched an inventory management system that allowed them to track their inventory more carefully.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=36)** With more careful inventory management, they didn't need as much inventory lying around idle in their warehouse stores.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=42)** - Less inventory lying around means fewer inventory purchases, and that means less cash flowing out to suppliers to pay for inventory that is just going to be sitting around on shelves.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=53)** - In 1985, Home Depot spent $69 million to buy extra inventory.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=58)** That contributed to their 1985 cashflow problems.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=62)** - In 1986, while still growing pretty rapidly, the company was able to keep that inventory increase down to $14 million, saving their cash for other things.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=71)** - [Instructor One] In 1986, Home Depot was also proactive in collecting its outstanding receivables.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=76)** - [Instructor Two] Receivables are a potential source of cash, and Home Depot certainly needed cash.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=81)** - [Instructor One] Home Depot reduced its overhead expenses, increased its profit margins, gently stretched out the time it took to pay its suppliers.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=88)** All of these things improved the company's operating cash flows and reduced the cash burn rate.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=94)** - Home Depot conducted an old-fashioned, roll up the sleeves, back to basics restruction of its operations, and it worked.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=102)** - In fact, with these operating cashflow improvements, along with some snazzy sales leaseback transactions, a story for another day, Home Depot was able to completely turn around its cashflow position.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=113)** - From a cash burn rate of $3 million per week in 1985, Home Depot switched over to a cash generation rate of almost $1 million per week, an amazing turnaround.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=125)** - It was this cash flow turnaround that saved Home Depot and launched the company on its path to become number 33 in the Fortune 500 listing in 2014.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=135)** - And how about the company's cash burn or cash generation rate now?
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=139)** - In 2014, Home Depot generated average surplus cash flow from operations over and above the cash needed for capital expenditures of $130 million a week.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-home-depot-story-revisited-14330683?u=76281980&t=151)** - It's all about cash flow, baby.
