>
> **Speakers:** - in (5), - [instructor (3), - it (2), - when (1), - so (1)

#### Managing operating cash flow
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=0)** - For a company that sells a product, the company's operating cycle is the amount of time that elapses from when the company buys the inventory until when the company collects the cash from selling that inventory.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=12)** The length of the operating cycle is the sum of the length of two very different processes.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=17)** First, how long from when a company buys the inventory until it sells or uses that inventory?
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=23)** Second, how long from when a company makes a sale until the company collects the cash from that sale?
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=29)** Let's talk about that first length of time, which is often called the number of days sales and inventory.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=34)** Let me illustrate with an example.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=36)** Nike makes athletic shoes and other sportswear.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=40)** Nike manufacturers these items and then sells them to retail outlets, typically on credit.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=45)** In terms of the length of Nike's operating cycle, we must first consider how many days elapsed from the time that Nike buys raw materials until the time that Nike sells the finished shoes and sportswear to retail outlets.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=58)** Using data from Nike's financial reports, it can be computed that this is about 80 days.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=63)** This is called the number of days sales and inventory.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=66)** We must end determine how long until Nike collects the cash from these sales to retail outlets.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=71)** On average, this is about 50 days.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=73)** We call this the average collection period.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=76)** If you take these two numbers and add them together, 80 days from purchase of raw materials to the sale of the finished goods and sportswear, and then 50 days from the sale to the cash collection, you get 130 days, that's the length of Nike's operating cycle.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=91)** Again, the operating cycle is the time from the purchase of the raw materials to the collection of the cash from the sale of the finished goods.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=98)** For Nike, that's 130 days.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=100)** When Nike purchases its raw materials on credit from its suppliers, Nike pays those bills off in about 40 days.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=107)** So Nike buys raw materials on credit and then pays for those raw materials after about 40 days.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=114)** But Nike doesn't collect the cash from selling the finished shoes and sportswear until a total of 130 days have passed.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=121)** This is why companies such as Nike must carefully manage purchases, sales and cash collections in order to balance out this mismatch between the timing of cash payments and the timing of cash collections.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=132)** If Nike wishes to manage its operating cash flow, the company has three dials it can turn.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=138)** First one is the number of days sales and inventory.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=141)** In a manufacturing company such as Nike, reducing the number of days sales and inventory involves streamlining the production process to get materials through the process as quickly as possible.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=151)** The longer it takes to make the shoes after buying the materials, then the longer Nike has to wait until collecting the cash from the sale of the shoes.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=159)** Second dial, average collection period.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=162)** If Nike allows its customers, the retail stores, to delay paying for their purchases, that means that Nike must borrow cash in order to pay its own bills while waiting for the customer cash.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=173)** There is a fine balance here.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=175)** Balancing the desire to collect the cash with a desire to please the customer by not pestering for cash payment too quickly.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=182)** The third dial is the number of days purchases in accounts payable.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=186)** Managing operating cashflow sometimes means stretching out the time when you pay your suppliers.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=191)** Again, this is a balancing act.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=193)** If you can stretch out this time, which averages 40 days for Nike, then you are preserving your cash to pay for other things.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=200)** However, you don't want to anger your suppliers by keeping them waiting too long.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=205)** If we put all the numbers together, it takes Nike 130 days to convert purchased raw materials into operating cash collections, 80 days to make and sell the product, and 50 days to collect the cash.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=217)** In the meantime, Nike pays for those raw materials in about 40 days.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=222)** So for 90 days, the difference between 130 days and 40 days, Nike has to use cash from other sources to keep its operation going.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=230)** Of course, in a steady state, Nike and other companies can use cash collected from prior sales to pay for the materials purchased to make goods for future sales.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=239)** But in a growing business where cash payments to make goods for future sales are greater than cash collected from past sales, this operating cycle mismatch can create large operating cash flow shortfalls.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=252)** Now to see a different operating cash flow pattern, let's talk about the cash operating cycle at McDonald's.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=257)** I love the cash flow pattern of McDonald's.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=260)** From the time that McDonald's buys raw materials, food and packaging, until McDonald's sells the finished product, the food wrapped in the packaging, about seven days elapse.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=271)** Cash collection takes about two seconds, because even when the customer uses a credit or debit card, McDonald's gets its electronic cash almost instantly.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=281)** Now, if McDonald's pays its suppliers under standard credit terms, McDonald's will pay for the food and packaging in about 30 days.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=289)** So for 23 days, 30 days minus the seven days and two seconds, McDonald's has the cash before needing to pay its own suppliers.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-operating-cash-flow-14328743?u=76281980&t=298)** This means that with this pattern of operating cash flows, McDonald's or another company with a similar pattern could grow as fast as it wanted with the operating cycle itself providing all the cash to finance the operating growth.

#### Managing investing cash flow
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=1)** - Capital expenditures are purchases of long-term operating assets, such as land, buildings, and equipment.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=6)** These assets are acquired to be used over the course of several years.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=10)** In the statement of cash flows, we classify the purchase of these assets as investing activities.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=15)** The value of long-term operating assets stems from the fact that they help companies generate future cash flows.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=21)** Capital budgeting is the name given to the process, whereby decisions are made about acquiring long-term operating assets.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=29)** There are several financial models used to make capital budgeting decisions.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=33)** A simple one is called payback period and involves computing how many years it will take to recover the initial investment cost.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=40)** Net present value, or NPV analysis, involves comparing the cost of the asset with the value of the expected cash inflows, after adjusting for the time value of money.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=50)** The value of a long-term operating asset can disappear instantly if events lower expectations about the future cash flows the asset can generate.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=58)** These computations are very interesting, but are beyond the scope of this course.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=62)** Now, capital budgeting decisions, such as the purchase of operating assets or the purchase of another company, are basically cash flow decisions.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=71)** The company gives up a certain amount of cash flows today, the purchase price, and in the future, operating outlays, in the hope of receiving a greater amount of cash flows in the future, as the asset generates revenues, and/or produces cost savings.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=86)** To illustrate a simple capital budgeting decision, assume that Yosef Manufacturing makes joysticks and other computer game accessories.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=94)** Yosef is considering expanding its operations by buying an additional production facility.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=100)** The cost of the new factory is $100 million.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=102)** This is an investing activity.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=104)** Yosef expects to be able to sell the joysticks and other items made in the factory for $80 million per year.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=111)** At that level of production, the annual cost of operating the factory, the wages, the insurance, the materials, the maintenance, and so forth, is expected to total $65 million.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=121)** The factory is expected to remain in operation for about 20 years.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=125)** Should Yosef buy this new factory for $100 million?
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=128)** That's an investing activity decision.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=130)** To summarize, Yosef must decide whether to pay $100 million for a factory that will generate a net cash inflow of $15 million per year, the 80 million inflow and the 65 million outflow for 20 years.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=143)** Now, one simple method that can be used to analyze this purchase is called the payback method or the payback period.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=149)** The payback period is just the time it takes for the company to recover its original investment, calculated by dividing the original investment cost by the net annual cash inflows received from the investment.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=161)** In the case of the factory purchase, being considered by Yosef Manufacturing, the payback period is 6.67 years, computed as follows.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=169)** The payback period is the initial investment divided by the annual inflow.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=173)** That's a $100 million divided by $15 million per year.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=176)** That 6.67 years, that's how it takes Yosef to get its money back.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=181)** One way to use this payback period number is to invest only in assets that pay back within a certain period of time, perhaps within five or six years.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=192)** This required payback period might be set differently for different types of assets.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=196)** In addition, qualitative factors, such as strategic positioning for future expansion and potential positive motivational effects on employees would influence the decision.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=207)** The important concept to remember here is that long-term operating assets have value because they are expected to help a company generate cash flows in the future.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=216)** If events occur to change this expectation, concerning those future cash flows, then the value of the asset changes.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=222)** For example, if consumer demand for computer joysticks dries up, the value of a factory built to produce joysticks can plunge overnight, even though the factory itself is still as productive as it ever was.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=235)** In addition to analysis of whether a certain investing activity cash investment is a good idea, a manager must also assess the timing of the investment to fit current cash management needs.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=247)** Some of the questions that need to be addressed are as follows.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=250)** Is current cash flow from operating activities, or through financing activities, such as borrowing, sufficient to pay for an attractive investing activity this year?
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=260)** If not, should the acquisition of the land, building, or equipment be delayed until next year?
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=266)** It is often efficient to acquire land, buildings, and equipment, in amounts a bit larger than you need immediately, knowing that you will grow into them in future years.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=274)** This can be better than expanding your productive capacity a little bit each year, for example.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=278)** So are current cash flows sufficient to pay for a large amount of investing activities this year?
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=284)** You see that managing investing cash flow requires you to think about your current operating and financing cash flows.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-investing-cash-flow-14329776?u=76281980&t=290)** A company's cash is a unifying element that links all aspects of a business, operating, investing, and financing.

#### Managing financing cash flow
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=0)** - The two sources of cash from financing activities are borrowing and owner investment.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=6)** The most desirable sequence of decision-making with respect to cash from financing activities is as follows: forecast the level of activity, such as the level of sales.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=16)** Evaluate the amount of cash that will be generated from or consumed by operating activities.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=22)** After a careful capital budgeting process, determine the amount of cash that will be needed for investing activities.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=29)** And finally, if financing cash inflows are needed, compare the relative costs of obtaining cash from borrowing and cash from owner investment.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=37)** As a general statement, the interest rate cost of borrowing money is lower than the cost of giving up ownership of part of the company in exchange for new partner investment or new shareholder investment.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=49)** The exact decision this year of whether it's best to obtain financing cash inflow from borrowing or from owner investment depends on market conditions this year.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=59)** Detailed consideration of these decisions is part of the field of finance.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=64)** An important cash outflow from financing activities is the payment of cash dividends.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=69)** A key question in the field of corporate finance is, why do companies pay cash dividends?
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=74)** Most established companies pay dividends.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=78)** Companies are reluctant to increase dividends.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=80)** They want to make sure that they'll be able to permanently sustain any increased level of dividends.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=85)** Cutting dividends is viewed as very bad news.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=89)** And finally, dividends seem to be used to give the illusion of stability.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=94)** If companies can get investors and potential investors to focus on their steady dividends rather than their volatile net income, then the companies look much less risky.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=104)** It's the illusion of stability.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=107)** In terms of managing financing cash flows, it is very important for a company to manage its relationships with providers of financing, lenders, current investors, and potential future investors.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=119)** Here is a story to illustrate how important these relationships are.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=123)** Years ago, I was allowed to sit in and observe a bank loan committee meeting.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=128)** Now, when I walked in the room, I was among the first ones there.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=130)** I started to sit down at a chair around a big table in the middle of the room.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=135)** I was gently reprimanded by a staff person who told me that only committee members were allowed to sit at the table.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=141)** I could join the staff and others sitting in one of the chairs up against the wall.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=145)** Okay, understood.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=147)** At the end of the meeting, which was a very interesting meeting, I stayed behind and was able to have a few words with the chairperson of the loan committee.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=153)** I asked him, "What are the most important factors that you look at in making a loan decision?"
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=159)** He said he was happy to tell me.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=160)** He said there are three factors.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=162)** And he told me them in reverse order.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=164)** He said, "Number three most important, the financial statements."
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=167)** Now this was disappointing to me because I, as an accounting professor, was under the mistaken impression that the numbers, the financial statement numbers are everything, liquidity measures and cashflow projections and so forth.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=178)** Now, he said, "Not number one, those are number three."
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=182)** Second most important thing he looks at, he said is the quality of the collateral, the building, or the machine, or the inventory or whatever that would be taken back by the bank and used to repay the loan if the borrower could not repay it from business cash flow.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=196)** And then he said the number one most important thing he considers in making a loan decision, personal character of the borrower.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=203)** He said that people of high character will work as long as it takes to pay back a loan, years if necessary.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=209)** In contrast, people of low character will immediately begin thinking of ways to not pay back the loan.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/managing-financing-cash-flow-14332591?u=76281980&t=215)** So in addition to forecasting cash flows and capital budgeting needs, an important part of managing financing activities is to maintain your credibility with outsiders from whom you will have to request those financing cash inflows.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
> **Best Practices:** it's best to (1)

#### Analyzing General Motors’ free cash flow
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=1)** - We can learn a lot about the ups and downs of General Motors over the past 15 years by looking at the company's free cash flow.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=7)** Here are General Motors free cash flow numbers for the years 1999 through 2001.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=12)** Now look at the cash flow numbers from 1999 through 2001, we have operating cash flow reported here.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=18)** Is it the direct or the indirect method?
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=21)** Well, it's the indirect. How can we tell?
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=23)** Because the presentation starts with net income.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=26)** If we're using the direct method, the first number is cash collected from customers.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=30)** Now to simplify this presentation, all of the cash flow implications of the changes in the balance sheet operating items, the accounts receivable, the inventory, the accounts payable, the prepaid expenses, the wages payable, all those things have been lumped together into one number.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=45)** Now to test your intuition, think of this one number as impact from change in inventory.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=50)** So, question: in 2001, did inventory increase or did it decrease?
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=56)** Well, the answer is that inventory increased in 2001, which means a reduction in operating cash flow.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=62)** More inventory means we have less cash flow.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=65)** Also, we have here the computation of free cash flow, operating cash flow and investing cash flow put together.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=72)** For a normal operating company, capital expenditures are by far the biggest component of investing cash flow.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=79)** So let's consider a couple of questions looking at these numbers here for General Motors.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=83)** First, we see that free cash flow in 1999 was $19.561 billion positive.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=90)** Now what could General Motors do with this free cash flow?
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=94)** Well, the answer is they can do anything, hence the name free cash flow.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=98)** They could repay loans. They could pay cash dividends.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=100)** They could repurchase their own shares or they can make higher future capital expenditures.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=104)** If we had been standing in early 2000, looking into the future of General Motors, we would have seen a company with huge possibilities.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=112)** A company with $20 billion in free cash flow in one year can do anything.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=117)** Now consider another question: in order to maintain its cash dividend payments in 2001, what did General Motors probably have to do?
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=125)** Well, with substantially lower free cash flow in that year, General Motors almost certainly had to borrow in 2001 in order to maintain its cash dividend payments.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=134)** In fact, in 1999, General Motors paid $1.37 billion in dividends.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=139)** With net income of $6 billion, we would have predicted something like this, maybe a little higher.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=144)** Now, where did they get the cash to pay those dividends in 1999?
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=147)** No problem, from free cash flow, they had $20 billion in free cash flow.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=152)** But to maintain these dividends in 2001, they would have had to borrow money.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=156)** In just two short years, we see General Motors going from being a company with unlimited possibilities to being a company that's borrowing money to pay its dividends.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=165)** Yikes.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=166)** What happened during those two years?
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=167)** Well, that would be an interesting thing to investigate, certainly beyond the scope of this course, but what we can see is that the whole nasty business is summarized quite nicely in a few cash flow numbers.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=178)** Now let's look at some General Motors free cash flow numbers from a few years later.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=182)** Look at these disgusting cash flow numbers and consider the following question: what's your forecast?

> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=188)** How long can General Motors survive with this free cash flow pattern?

> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=194)** You can't burn $20 billion of cash every year and last very long.

> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/analyzing-general-motors-free-cash-flow?u=76281980&t=203)** Well, given the free cash flow numbers we see from 2005 through 2008, we are not at all surprised to learn that this company declared bankruptcy in the very next year.

#### Apple: Cash flow data helps unravel accounting issues
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=1)** - Let's see how cash flow analysis can help us see through the impact of opaque accounting rules.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=6)** Before 2009, Apple was required to record revenue from its iPhone sales, using something called subscription accounting.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=13)** Under this approach, Apple was required to spread the revenue for an iPhone sale for say $600, evenly over the entire life of the transaction, because each iPhone was accompanied by a promise of free future software upgrades, the iPhone sale was not a one and done thing.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=31)** At least not according to the accounting rules.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=33)** With a four year expected life of an iPhone, Apple would have had to drag out the iPhone revenue $150 per year for a phone that had already been delivered to a customer years before.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=44)** If this sounds weird to you, you're in good company, lots of people were mystified about an accounting rule that would not let Apple immediately report the sale of a $600 iPhone.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=54)** It seems like a straightforward transaction.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=57)** The nebulous promise of future free software upgrades shouldn't change the reporting of the sale, but it did.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=63)** Apple had collected billions of dollars from selling millions of iPhones, but the accounting rules in place at the time wouldn't let Apple report to sales.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=74)** So Apple's income statement wasn't really reporting what was going on.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=78)** In 2009, the accounting rules were changed so that apple could report almost all of the iPhone sales amounts immediately.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=85)** This change had a substantial impact on Apple's reported income statement results.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=91)** To highlight this impact, Apple restated its results for 2008 and 2009.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=96)** Here's a table that apple presented that shows selected financial statement numbers before and after the accounting change.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=104)** A focus on the year ended September 26th, 2009, with the old rules under which revenue from the sale of a single iPhone was spread out evenly over as long as four years, total reported revenue was $36.537 billion.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=119)** With the new accounting rules, allowing Apple to report almost all of the iPhone sales amount immediately, reported revenue was 42.905 billion.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=128)** A 70% increase, and this all is because of an accounting difference.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=132)** So with no change in underlying economic activity, the reported sales of Apple were 17% higher using improved accounting rules, focusing on the delivery of economic value to customers.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=144)** The difference in net income is even greater.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=147)** Net income in 2009, under the old subscription accounting revenue recognition method was $5.704 billion.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=155)** With the improved approach, reported net income was 8.235 billion, a 44% increase.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=161)** 44%, economics were still the same, but with a change in accounting rules, the company has a 44% increase in net income.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=169)** Obviously accounting rules and accounting assumptions can substantially impact a company's performance as reported on paper.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=176)** Now, is there any way to peek behind the impact of these accounting rules and accounting assumptions, basically go around the accounts, cash flow to the rescue, take a look at the operating cashflow numbers under both the old accounting rules and the new ones, Apple's operating cashflow in 2009 is $10.159 billion.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=196)** Operating cashflow is not impacted by accounting assumptions.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=199)** As mentioned previously, the operating cashflow numbers can serve as a reality check when you have some concerns about the reported net income number.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=207)** This Apple situation is a good illustration during those years when people were shaking their heads about the mystifying subscription accounting rules that Apple was forced to use, the informed investor was calmly turning the page in the annual report to see the operating cashflow number and to find out what was really going on.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=225)** Now, as long as we're talking about Apple's operating cashflow, let's talk about world records.

> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=229)** We mentioned earlier that Exxon mobile holds the world record.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=232)** As far as we know for the most operating cashflow generated in one year, that was in 2008, $59.725 billion.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=241)** In 2014, there was some excitement among those of us, strange people, who pay attention to this kind of thing.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/apple-cash-flow-data-helps-unravel-accounting-issues?u=76281980&t=247)** Apple was getting close, strong operating cashflow performance suggested that with a good fourth quarter, Apple could break ExxonMobil's all time record.

#### The danger of free cash flow
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=1)** - A key issue in corporate governance is the fact that the owners of the corporation, the shareholders, do not actually run the corporation.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=8)** Instead, the owners hire, through the elected board of directors, professional managers to run the corporation.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=13)** Problems arise because the economic interest of the owners and the professional managers are not exactly aligned.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=20)** For example, from the standpoint of the shareholders, there is some economically optimal amount of money to spend on corporate golf outings because crucial stakeholder relationships can sometimes be developed and enhanced on the golf course.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=33)** So the shareholders are happy to pay for some amount of golfing by the chief executive officer and the other professional managers.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=40)** However, what if the CEO doesn't view these golf outings as a corporate chore, but actually instead likes golfing at exotic locations?
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=49)** In this case, the CEO may spend more corporate resources on golf than is justified by the resulting economic benefit to the shareholder.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=58)** The CEO is set to be consuming management perquisites, often just called perks.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=63)** The problem of excess consumption of management perks is increased when a company is generating surplus cash flows.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=71)** These surplus cash flows are what we have called free cash flow and could be thought of as the extra cash flow generated by operations over and above what is needed to keep the corporation running and expanding.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=83)** As you recall, computationally, free cash flow is the amount of operating cash flow, less the amount of investing cashflow.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=90)** Because the corporation doesn't need this surplus cash to proceed with its planned expansion efforts, the cash should be returned to the owners, the shareholders, to use in whatever way they see fit.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=102)** After all, the surplus cash belongs to the shareholders.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=106)** This problem has been summarized as follows by famous professors, Jensen and Meckling.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=113)** "Conflicts of interest between shareholders and managers over payout policies are especially severe when the organization generates substantial free cash flow.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=122)** The problem is how to motivate managers to disgorge the cash rather than investing it at below the cost of capital or wasting it on organizational inefficiencies."
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=133)** A classic example of the inefficient use of free cashflow is seen in the case of RJR Nabisco.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=139)** In October, 1988, RJR Nabisco, which is a combination of the cigarette company, RJ Reynolds, and the food company, Nabisco, inventors of Fig Newtons and Oreos, was a candidate for a leveraged buyout and LBO because of its attractive cash flows.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=156)** These cash flows could be used to pay the interest on the debt incurred in the LBO.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=161)** The public bidding for the company started at $72 per share.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=164)** This bid was put forward by a group, including the existing management team.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=168)** However, as the company's operations were scrutinized more carefully, it became apparent that with more efficient use of corporate resources, the company could be worth much more.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=178)** Because of ready availability for surplus cash flow, the management of RJR Nabisco was paying extra millions for golf tournaments and Marquis athletic consulting contracts.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=189)** The company had shelled out extra tens of millions on corporate jets and on an elaborate corporate air terminal and extra billions on new, perhaps unnecessary technology upgrades.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=200)** This exorbitant was simply a way to spend the surplus cash that the company didn't know what to do with.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=206)** When the impact of the elimination of all these inefficiencies was factored in, the final LBO price was not $72 per share, but $108 per share.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=216)** At this price, the total cost of the LBO was $20 billion, making the RJR Nabisco deal the largest of the leveraged buyouts of the late 1980s.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=225)** Free cash flow belongs to the owners.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-cash-flow/the-danger-of-free-cash-flow-14327835?u=76281980&t=228)** One of the challenges in a successful company is to reduce the amount of this free cash flow that is wasted by the company management.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** rjr (4), lbo (4), ceo (3)
> **Code Keywords:** case, (1), public (1), new, (1)
> **Tools:** terminal (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Speakers:** - a (1)
